<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Kamala Harris that "because of our economic policies we now are reducing inflation" is partially supported by the search results. The Inflation Reduction Act, which was passed in 2022 and implemented by the Biden administration, includes provisions aimed at reducing inflation, such as increasing energy efficiency, investing in clean energy, and lowering healthcare costs.

According to the Congressional Budget Office (CBO) estimates, the Inflation Reduction Act is expected to reduce cumulative deficits by $248 billion over the next decade, which could help to reduce inflation. Additionally, the Penn Wharton Budget Model (PWBM) estimated that the Act would have no impact on gross domestic product (GDP) in 2031.

However, it is unclear whether the economic policies implemented by the Biden administration are the sole cause of the reduction in inflation. Other factors, such as changes in global economic conditions, monetary policy, and supply chain disruptions, may also be contributing to the reduction in inflation.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Ron DeSantis that "Biden's let in 8 million people just in four years" is likely an exaggeration or misrepresentation of the actual number of immigrants who have entered the United States during the Biden administration.
According to the search results, the total foreign-born population in the United States has increased by 4.5 million since President Biden took office, but this number includes both legal and illegal immigrants. The Census Bureau's Current Population Survey (CPS) shows that the total foreign-born population was 49.5 million in October 2023, which is a new record high.
However, the number of immigrants who have entered the United States during the Biden administration is not as clear-cut. The U.S. Customs and Border Protection (CBP) reported 2.5 million encounters of migrants at the U.S.-Mexico border in fiscal year 2023, which is a new historic high. However, this number includes both repeat crossers and individuals who have been apprehended multiple times.
The number of immigrants who have been deported or removed from the United States during the Biden administration is also not as high as 8 million. According to the U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE) annual report, the agency deported 142,580 immigrants in fiscal year 2023, which is nearly double the number from the year before.

2. rfsl.se: In Sweden you are assigned your legal gender at birth by healthcare staff determining your gender based on outer genitalia.

According to the search results, the African American unemployment rate did reach a record low under the Trump administration, with a rate of 5.4% in August 2019, according to politico.com. However, this rate was not the lowest ever recorded, as the unemployment rate for Black workers was even lower under President Biden, according to presidency.ucsb.edu.
Regarding the poverty rate, the African American poverty rate did reach a record low in 2019, with a rate of 18.8%, according to bbc.com. However, this rate was not the lowest ever recorded, as the poverty rate for Black Americans was lower under President Obama, according to bbc.com.
Therefore, the claim made by Donald Trump is mostly-false, as it exaggerates the achievements of the Trump administration in reducing African American unemployment and poverty rates.

 1. kff.org: The prescription drug provisions included in the Inflation Reduction Act will: Require the federal government to negotiate prices for some drugs covered under Medicare Part B and Part D with the highest total spending, beginning in 2026 Require drug companies to pay rebates to Medicare if prices rise faster than inflation for drugs used by Medicare beneficiaries, beginning in 2023 Cap out-of-pocket spending for Medicare Part D enrollees and make other Part D benefit design changes, beginning in 2024 Limit monthly cost sharing for insulin to $35 for people with Medicare, beginning in 2023 Eliminate cost sharing for adult vaccines covered under Medicare Part D and improve access to adult vaccines in Medicaid and CHIP, beginning in 2023 Expand eligibility for full benefits under the Medicare Part D Low-Income Subsidy Program, beginning in 2024 Further delay implementation of the Trump Administration’s drug rebate rule, beginning in 2027 This brief summarizes these provisions and discusses the expected effects on people, program spending, and drug prices and innovation. 
 Effects on the Development of New Drugs CBO estimates that the drug pricing provisions in the Inflation Reduction Act, including but not limited to the new Medicare drug price negotiation program, will have a very modest impact on the number of new drugs coming to market in the U.S. over the next 30 years: 13 fewer out of 1,300, or a reduction of 1% (about 1 fewer drug over the 2023-2032 period, about 5 fewer drugs in the subsequent decade, and about 7 fewer drugs in the decade after that). 
 budgetary impact CBO estimates $98.5 billion in Medicare savings over 10 years (2022-2031) from the drug negotiation provisions in the Inflation Reduction Act.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Joe Biden that the federal deficit was reduced by $160 billion due to Medicare no longer having to pay exorbitant prices to Big Pharma is partially supported by the search results. The Congressional Budget Office (CBO) estimates that the prescription drug provisions in the Inflation Reduction Act will result in $98.5 billion in Medicare savings over 10 years (2022-2031) from the drug negotiation provisions. However, the CBO also estimates that the deficit for 2024 is $0.1 trillion (or 4 percent) smaller than it was in the agency's May 2023 projections, and the cumulative deficit over the 2024–2033 period is smaller by $1.4 trillion (or 7 percent). This suggests that the deficit reduction is likely due to a combination of factors, including the prescription drug provisions, rather than solely due to Medicare no longer paying exorbitant prices to Big Pharma.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Mike Pence that the US achieved energy independence in three short years is likely an exaggeration or misrepresentation of the facts.
According to the Energy Information Administration (EIA), the US has made significant progress in increasing its energy production and reducing its reliance on foreign oil, but it is still not energy independent. The EIA's Short-Term Energy Outlook (STEO) reports that the US is expected to continue to rely on imports to meet a significant portion of its energy needs, particularly for oil and natural gas.
Additionally, the EIA's data shows that the US has not achieved energy independence in the past three years. In fact, the US still relies on imports to meet a significant portion of its energy needs, and the country's energy trade balance is still a net importer of energy.
The claim made by Mike Pence is also contradicted by the fact that the US is still a significant importer of oil and natural gas, and that the country's energy trade balance is still a net importer of energy.

2. cis.org: Skip to main content Home Andrew R. Arthur More than 1.6 Million Pending Asylum Applications By Andrew R. Arthur on August 16, 2023 Halfway through FY 2023 (through March) there were a total of more than 1.6 million pending asylum applications in the United States, in two different buckets: nearly 800,000 "defensive" asylum claims awaiting adjudication before fewer than 650 immigration judges in the nation’s immigration courts, with the other 842,000 being "affirmative" asylum applications in the USCIS backlog for decisions by fewer than 850 asylum officers , as my colleague Elizabeth Jacobs recently reported . 
 By contrast, more than 256,000 asylum applications were filed with the immigration courts in FY 2022, and you can add 196,000 more that were filed in the first six months of FY 2023 – at a rate six times as high as nine years prior. 
 It shows that in the first two quarters of FY 2023, immigration judges decided 103,241 asylum claims; in other words, about 46 percent of the removal cases decided in that period, as described in the first chart, involved asylum.

 1. kff.org: Average out-of-pocket spending per insulin prescription was $54 in 2020 – over 50% more than the proposed $35 monthly copay cap for insulin – but spending on many insulin products was higher Among Medicare Part D insulin users who do not receive low-income subsidies, average out-of-pocket costs per prescription across all insulin products was $54 in 2020 – over 50% more than the proposed $35 monthly copay cap for insulin. 
 Among insulin users, Part D enrollees without low-income subsidies spent $572 per person for insulin in 2020, on average, but a small share of insulin users spent considerably more Among insulin users without Part D low-income subsidies (LIS), average annual out-of-pocket spending on insulin per user increased by 76% between 2007 and 2020, from $324 to $572 (Figure 3; see Table 1 for 2020 insulin use and spending by state). 
 Legislation has been introduced in Congress that would cap monthly copayments for insulin products at $35 for people with Medicare and private insurance, require the federal government to negotiate insulin prices , and encourage insulin manufacturers to lower list prices. 
2. aspe.hhs.gov: Effective January 1, 2023, out-of-pocket costs for insulin are capped at $35 per monthly prescription among Medicare Part D enrollees under the Inflation Reduction Act (IRA). 
 An estimated 1.5 million Medicare beneficiaries who use insulin would have saved $734 million in Part D and $27 million in Part B if these caps had been in effect in 2020. 
 Related Content: Report to Congress on the Affordability of Insulin Files Document Insulin Affordability IRA Data Point (pdf, 384.92 KB)

 The amount spent out-of-pocket for insulin has skyrocketed for Medicare enrollees , from $236 million in 2007 to more than $1 billion in 2020. 
4. medicarerights.org: Prior to the IRA’s changes, the national average out-of-pocket cost for a month’s supply of insulin for people with Medicare or private insurance was about $63 per fill.

 Coinsurances and copays for insulin covered under Part D have been limited to $35 a month for a month’s supply of each medication since January 1.

<user> Claim: U.S. aid to Ukraine will continue during a government shutdown, but programs for food stamps, transportation, education, justice, environmental protection and homelessness will not.. Made by Instagram post. Date: 2023-09-22. 
<assistant> Reasoning: The claim that U.S. aid to Ukraine will continue during a government shutdown, but programs for food stamps, transportation, education, justice, environmental protection, and homelessness will not, is partially true.
According to the U.S. government's shutdown contingency plans, certain essential functions, including national security and defense, will continue to operate during a shutdown. This includes funding for military operations, including aid to Ukraine.
However, non-essential functions, including many social services and programs, will be suspended or significantly reduced during a shutdown. This includes programs such as food stamps (Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program, or SNAP), transportation, education, justice, environmental protection, and homelessness services.
The claim is partially true because it accurately reflects the distinction between essential and non-essential functions during a government shutdown. However, it is not entirely accurate because it implies that all non-essential programs will be completely shut down, when in fact some may continue to operate with reduced staff and resources.
Final answer: mostly-true
Label extracted:  mostly true . True label:  mixture

 According to a 2015 article by the sociologist and criminal justice professor Paul Hirschfield, these include: the arming of police (which is not routine in Norway and Iceland, though it is in Finland), a higher prevalence of guns among the general public ("American police are primed to expect guns"), decentralized and under-resourced police training (although Hirschfield does not mention the duration of training as a factor), and racial biases (whether conscious or unconscious) among law enforcement officers.

 In this rapid living systematic evidence synthesis and meta-analysis, we searched EMBASE and the US National Institutes of Health's iSearch COVID-19 Portfolio, supplemented by manual searches of COVID-19-specific sources, until Dec 1, 2022, for studies that reported vaccine effectiveness immediately and at least 112 days after a primary vaccine series or at least 84 days after a booster dose.

4. ecdc.europa.eu: Copy citation to clipboard This report presents pooled COVID-19 vaccine effectiveness (VE) estimates for the first, second and third booster doses, against hospitalisation due to COVID-19 and COVID-19 related deaths in resident populations ≥50 years of age, living in the community, in six European countries: Belgium, Denmark, Luxembourg, Navarre (Spain), Norway, and Portugal. 
 Compared to complete primary vaccination, the first booster dose rVE against hospitalisation due to COVID-19 was mostly ≤50% between April 2022 and March 2023 in all age groups (with a few point estimates >50%). 
 Compared to complete primary vaccination, rVE of a second booster restored protection shortly after administration in the autumn of 2022 in ≥65-year-olds, to 76–79% against hospitalisation due to COVID-19 and to 76–85% against COVID-19 related death.

 In this scenario, the main objective of our study was to assess the joint protective effect of a previous SARS-CoV-2 infection and vaccination on the risk of new infections and hospitalizations for COVID-19 in the real-world setting exploiting the data available in the administrative database of the LHU of Vercelli, Piedmont, Italy.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Ron DeSantis that "Every booster you take, you're more likely to get COVID as a result of it" is not supported by the search results. In fact, the search results suggest that booster doses of COVID-19 vaccines are effective in preventing severe illness and hospitalization due to COVID-19, and that the effectiveness of booster doses can vary depending on the specific vaccine and the population being studied.

The search results from respiratory-research.biomedcentral.com, ncbi.nlm.nih.gov, thelancet.com, ecdc.europa.eu, and nature.com all suggest that booster doses of COVID-19 vaccines can provide protection against COVID-19, although the level of protection may decrease over time.

There is no evidence in the search results to support the claim that every booster dose increases the risk of getting COVID-19. In fact, the opposite is true: booster doses are intended to provide additional protection against COVID-19, and the search results suggest that they can be effective in doing so.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Tim Scott that parents who show up at school board meetings are called "domestic terrorists" under the DOJ is partially true. The National School Boards Association (NSBA) did write a letter to the White House and the DOJ in September 2021, asking them to invoke the Patriot Act to address threats against school officials, which included parents who were speaking out at school board meetings. The letter referred to these parents as "domestic terrorists."

However, the DOJ did not directly label parents as "domestic terrorists." Instead, the DOJ issued a memo in October 2021, directing US Attorneys to convene meetings to learn more about threats against school officials, and to work with local law enforcement to address violence and threats of violence.

It's worth noting that the NSBA's letter was widely criticized, and many lawmakers and advocacy groups condemned the association's characterization of parents as "domestic terrorists."

Final answer: mostly-true

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Donald Trump that "They used COVID to cheat" is a false statement. There is no evidence to support the claim that the COVID-19 pandemic was used by Democrats to cheat in the 2020 presidential election. In fact, the COVID-19 pandemic was a global health crisis that affected many countries, including the United States, and was not a deliberate attempt to cheat in an election.
The claim is also contradicted by reputable sources such as the World Health Organization (WHO), the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), and the US intelligence community, which have all stated that the COVID-19 pandemic was a natural occurrence and not a deliberate attempt to cheat in an election.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim that the Pfizer COVID-19 vaccine "shuts off" recipients' hearts is an exaggeration and misinterpretation of the vaccine's effects. According to the search results, the vaccine is associated with a rare but heightened risk of acute myocarditis and pericarditis, which are inflammation of the heart muscle and the outer lining of the heart, respectively. However, these conditions are not caused by the vaccine "shutting off" the heart, but rather by an inflammatory response to the vaccine.

The search results from reputable sources such as the CDC, PubMed, and BMJ Group confirm that myocarditis and pericarditis are rare side effects of the Pfizer COVID-19 vaccine, and that they are more common in young males after the second dose. However, the symptoms of these conditions are not a result of the heart being "shut off," but rather chest pain, shortness of breath, and palpitations.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Kirsten Gillibrand that marijuana is currently classified in the same category of drugs as heroin and a more dangerous category than fentanyl or cocaine is false.
According to the search results, marijuana is currently classified as a Schedule I substance in the Controlled Substances Act (CSA), which means the federal government concluded that marijuana has no currently accepted medical use in any setting. However, on August 29, 2023, the U.S. Department of Health and Human Services recommended that marijuana be reclassified from a Schedule I controlled substance to a Schedule III controlled substance, which would indicate that marijuana has a "potential for abuse less" than Schedule I and II drugs and "has a currently accepted medical use in treatment in the United States."
Schedule I substances, such as heroin, are considered to have a high potential for abuse and no currently accepted medical use. Schedule III substances, such as anabolic steroids, are considered to have a lower potential for abuse and a currently accepted medical use.
Fentanyl is classified as a Schedule II substance, which is considered to have a high potential for abuse but also a currently accepted medical use.
Cocaine is classified as a Schedule II substance, which is considered to have a high potential for abuse but also a currently accepted medical use.

5. unstats.un.org: The 2020 World Population and Housing Census Programme comprises a number of inter-related objectives and activities aimed at ensuring that: Member States conduct at least one population and housing census during the period from 2015 to 2024; Member States take into account international and regional recommendations relating to population and housing censuses and give particular attention to advance planning, cost efficiency, coverage and the timely dissemination of, and easy access to, census results; Member States set quality standards for the conduct and evaluation of population and housing censuses in order to uphold the integrity, reliability, accuracy and value of the population and housing census results; Full-fledged support is rendered to the Programme given its importance for sustainable development planning, especially in the context of the 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development; International statistical standards, methods and guidelines are prepared to facilitate the conduct of population and housing censuses; Activities among stakeholders are coordinated in assisting Member States to plan and carry out a census; and, Implementation of the Programme is monitored and regularly reported to the Statistical Commission.
